   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    Menstrual cycle
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div class="noprint" id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
     Jump to:
     <a href="#mw-head">
      navigation
     </a>
     ,
     <a href="#p-search">
      search
     </a>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="mw-parser-output">
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">
        Menstruation
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Menstruation_(mammal)" title="Menstruation (mammal)">
        Menstruation (mammal)
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Figure_28_02_07.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1031" data-file-width="485" height="638" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Figure_28_02_07.jpg/300px-Figure_28_02_07.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Figure_28_02_07.jpg/450px-Figure_28_02_07.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f3/Figure_28_02_07.jpg 2x" width="300"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Figure_28_02_07.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Figure showing the progression of the menstrual cycle and the different hormones contributing to it.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The
       <b>
        menstrual cycle
       </b>
       is the regular natural change that occurs in the
       <a href="/wiki/Female_reproductive_system" title="Female reproductive system">
        female reproductive system
       </a>
       (specifically the
       <a href="/wiki/Uterus" title="Uterus">
        uterus
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Ovary" title="Ovary">
        ovaries
       </a>
       ) that makes
       <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">
        pregnancy
       </a>
       possible.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverthorn_1-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Silverthorn-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sherwood_2-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Sherwood-2">
         [2]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The cycle is required for the production of ovocytes, and for the preparation of the uterus for
       <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">
        pregnancy
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverthorn_1-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Silverthorn-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Up to 80% of women report having some symptoms during the one to two weeks prior to
       <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">
        menstruation
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP2011_3-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-AFP2011-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Common symptoms include
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Acne_vulgaris" title="Acne vulgaris">
        acne
       </a>
       , tender breasts, bloating, feeling tired, irritability and mood changes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women2014PMS_4-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Women2014PMS-4">
         [4]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These symptoms interfere with normal life and therefore qualify as
       <a href="/wiki/Premenstrual_syndrome" title="Premenstrual syndrome">
        premenstrual syndrome
       </a>
       in 20 to 30% of women. In 3 to 8%, they are severe.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AFP2011_3-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-AFP2011-3">
         [3]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The first period usually begins between twelve and fifteen years of age, a point in time known as
       <a href="/wiki/Menarche" title="Menarche">
        menarche
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2011_5-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jones2011-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They may occasionally start as early as eight, and this onset may still be normal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women2014Men_6-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Women2014Men-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The average age of the first period is generally later in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">
        developing world
       </a>
       and earlier in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_world" title="Developed world">
        developed world
       </a>
       . The typical length of time between the first day of one period and the first day of the next is 21 to 45 days in young women and 21 to 35 days in adults (an average of 28 days).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women2014Men_6-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Women2014Men-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diaz2006_7-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Diaz2006-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Menstruation stops occurring after
       <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">
        menopause
       </a>
       which usually occurs between 45 and 55 years of age.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-NIH2013Def_8-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-NIH2013Def-8">
         [8]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Bleeding usually lasts around 2 to 7 days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women2014Men_6-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Women2014Men-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The menstrual cycle is governed by hormonal changes.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women2014Men_6-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Women2014Men-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These changes can be altered by using
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormonal_birth_control" title="Hormonal birth control">
        hormonal birth control
       </a>
       to prevent pregnancy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
        <a href="#cite_note-9">
         [9]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Each cycle can be divided into three phases based on events in the ovary (ovarian cycle) or in the uterus (uterine cycle).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverthorn_1-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Silverthorn-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The ovarian cycle consists of the
       <a href="/wiki/Follicular_phase" title="Follicular phase">
        follicular phase
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">
        ovulation
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Luteal_phase" title="Luteal phase">
        luteal phase
       </a>
       whereas the uterine cycle is divided into
       <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">
        menstruation
       </a>
       , proliferative phase, and secretory phase.
      </p>
      <p>
       Stimulated by gradually increasing amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">
        estrogen
       </a>
       in the follicular phase, discharges of blood (menses) flow stop, and the
       <a href="/wiki/Endometrium" title="Endometrium">
        lining
       </a>
       of the uterus thickens.
       <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_follicle" title="Ovarian follicle">
        Follicles
       </a>
       in the ovary begin developing under the influence of a complex interplay of hormones, and after several days one or occasionally two become dominant (non-dominant follicles shrink and die). Approximately mid-cycle, 24–36 hours after the
       <a href="/wiki/Luteinizing_hormone" title="Luteinizing hormone">
        luteinizing hormone
       </a>
       (LH) surges, the dominant follicle releases an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovocyte" title="Ovocyte">
        ovocyte
       </a>
       , in an event called ovulation. After ovulation, the ovocyte only lives for 24 hours or less without fertilization while the remains of the dominant follicle in the ovary become a
       <a href="/wiki/Corpus_luteum" title="Corpus luteum">
        corpus luteum
       </a>
       ; this body has a primary function of producing large amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Progesterone" title="Progesterone">
        progesterone
       </a>
       . Under the influence of progesterone, the
       <a href="/wiki/Endometrium" title="Endometrium">
        uterine lining
       </a>
       changes to prepare for potential
       <a href="/wiki/Implantation_(human_embryo)" title="Implantation (human embryo)">
        implantation
       </a>
       of an embryo to establish a pregnancy. If implantation does not occur within approximately two weeks, the corpus luteum will involute, causing a sharp drop in levels of both progesterone and estrogen. The hormone drop causes the uterus to shed its lining in a process termed menstruation. Menstruation also occurs in some other animals including
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shrews" title="Shrews">
        shrews
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bats" title="Bats">
        bats
       </a>
       , and other
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Primates" title="Primates">
        primates
       </a>
       such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apes" title="Apes">
        apes
       </a>
       and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monkeys" title="Monkeys">
        monkeys
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kris2013_10-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kris2013-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">

             </span>
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </li>

      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Onset_and_frequency">
        Onset and frequency
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Onset and frequency">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lining_of_Uterine_Wall.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="269" data-file-width="383" height="155" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Lining_of_Uterine_Wall.jpg/220px-Lining_of_Uterine_Wall.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Lining_of_Uterine_Wall.jpg/330px-Lining_of_Uterine_Wall.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/9/9f/Lining_of_Uterine_Wall.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lining_of_Uterine_Wall.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Diagram illustrating how the
         <a href="/wiki/Endometrium" title="Endometrium">
          uterus lining
         </a>
         builds up and breaks down during the menstrual cycle.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The average age of menarche is 12–15.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jones2011_5-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Jones2011-5">
         [5]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
        <a href="#cite_note-11">
         [11]
        </a>
       </sup>
       They may occasionally start as early as eight, and this onset may still be normal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Women2014Men_6-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Women2014Men-6">
         [6]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This first period often occurs later in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developing_world" title="Developing world">
        developing world
       </a>
       than the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Developed_world" title="Developed world">
        developed world
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Diaz2006_7-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Diaz2006-7">
         [7]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The average age of menarche is approximately 12.5 years in the
       <a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">
        United States
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
        <a href="#cite_note-12">
         [12]
        </a>
       </sup>
       12.7 in
       <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">
        Canada
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
        <a href="#cite_note-13">
         [13]
        </a>
       </sup>
       12.9 in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/UK" title="UK">
        UK
       </a>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">
        <a href="#cite_note-14">
         [14]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and 13.1 years in
       <a href="/wiki/Iceland" title="Iceland">
        Iceland
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">
        <a href="#cite_note-15">
         [15]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Factors such as genetics, diet and overall health can affect timing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-age_16-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-age-16">
         [16]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The cessation of menstrual cycles at the end of a woman's reproductive period is termed
       <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">
        menopause
       </a>
       . The average age of menopause in women is 52 years, with anywhere between 45 and 55 being common. Menopause before age 45 is considered
       <i>
        premature
       </i>
       in industrialised countries.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
        <a href="#cite_note-17">
         [17]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Like the age of menarche, the age of menopause is largely a result of cultural and biological factors;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-beyene_18-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-beyene-18">
         [18]
        </a>
       </sup>
       however, illnesses, certain surgeries, or medical treatments may cause menopause to occur earlier than it might have otherwise.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">
        <a href="#cite_note-19">
         [19]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The length of a woman's menstrual cycle typically varies somewhat, with some shorter cycles and some longer cycles. A woman who experiences variations of less than eight days between her longest cycles and shortest cycles is considered to have regular menstrual cycles. It is unusual for a woman to experience cycle length variations of less than four days. Length variation between eight and 20 days is considered as moderately
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Irregular_cycles" title="Irregular cycles">
        irregular cycles
       </a>
       . Variation of 21 days or more between a woman's shortest and longest cycle lengths is considered very irregular.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kippley_20-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-kippley-20">
         [20]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The average menstrual cycle lasts 28 days. The variability of menstrual cycle lengths is highest for women under 25 years of age and is lowest, that is, most regular, for ages 25 to 39.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chiazze1968_21-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chiazze1968-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Subsequently, the variability increases slightly for women aged 40 to 44.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chiazze1968_21-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Chiazze1968-21">
         [21]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The luteal phase of the menstrual cycle is about the same length in most individuals (mean 14.13 days,
       <a href="/wiki/Standard_deviation" title="Standard deviation">
        standard deviation
       </a>
       1.41 days)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lenton_luteal_22-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lenton_luteal-22">
         [22]
        </a>
       </sup>
       whereas the follicular phase tends to show much more variability (log-normally distributed with 95% of individuals having follicular phases between 10.3 and 16.3 days).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lenton_follicular_23-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lenton_follicular-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The follicular phase also seems to get significantly shorter with age (geometric mean 14.2 days in women aged 18–24 vs. 10.4 days in women aged 40–44).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lenton_follicular_23-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lenton_follicular-23">
         [23]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">
        Health effects
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Health effects">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <p>
       Some women with
       <a href="/wiki/Neurology" title="Neurology">
        neurological conditions
       </a>
       experience increased activity of their conditions at about the same time during each menstrual cycle. For example, drops in estrogen levels have been known to trigger
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Migraines" title="Migraines">
        migraines
       </a>
       ,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
        <a href="#cite_note-24">
         [24]
        </a>
       </sup>
       especially when the woman who suffers migraines is also taking the birth control pill. Many women with
       <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">
        epilepsy
       </a>
       have more seizures in a pattern linked to the menstrual cycle; this is called "
       <a href="/wiki/Catamenial_epilepsy" title="Catamenial epilepsy">
        catamenial epilepsy
       </a>
       ".
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18164632_25-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pmid18164632-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Different patterns seem to exist (such as seizures coinciding with the time of menstruation, or coinciding with the time of ovulation), and the frequency with which they occur has not been firmly established. Using one particular definition, one group of scientists found that around one-third of women with intractable partial epilepsy has catamenial epilepsy.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid18164632_25-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-pmid18164632-25">
         [25]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid15349872_26-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pmid15349872-26">
         [26]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid9579954_27-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pmid9579954-27">
         [27]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An effect of hormones has been proposed, in which progesterone declines and estrogen increases would trigger seizures.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid16981857_28-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-pmid16981857-28">
         [28]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Recently, studies have shown that high doses of estrogen can cause or worsen seizures, whereas high doses of progesterone can act like an antiepileptic drug.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-epilepsy_29-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-epilepsy-29">
         [29]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Studies by medical journals have found that women experiencing menses are 1.68 times more likely to commit
       <a href="/wiki/Suicide" title="Suicide">
        suicide
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SuicideRatesPsyMed_30-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-SuicideRatesPsyMed-30">
         [30]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Mice have been used as an experimental system to investigate possible mechanisms by which levels of
       <a href="/wiki/Sex_steroid" title="Sex steroid">
        sex steroid
       </a>
       hormones might regulate
       <a href="/wiki/Nervous_system" title="Nervous system">
        nervous system
       </a>
       function. During the part of the mouse estrous cycle when progesterone is highest, the level of
       <a href="/wiki/Neuron" title="Neuron">
        nerve-cell
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GABA_A_receptor" title="GABA A receptor">
        GABA receptor
       </a>
       subtype delta was high. Since these GABA receptors are
       <a href="/wiki/Inhibitory_postsynaptic_potential" title="Inhibitory postsynaptic potential">
        inhibitory
       </a>
       , nerve cells with more delta receptors are less likely to fire than cells with lower numbers of delta receptors. During the part of the mouse estrous cycle when estrogen levels are higher than progesterone levels, the number of delta receptors decrease, increasing nerve cell activity, in turn increasing anxiety and seizure susceptibility.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Maguire_31-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Maguire-31">
         [31]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Estrogen levels may affect
       <a href="/wiki/Thyroid" title="Thyroid">
        thyroid
       </a>
       behavior.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
        <a href="#cite_note-32">
         [32]
        </a>
       </sup>
       For example, during the luteal phase (when estrogen levels are lower), the velocity of blood flow in the thyroid is lower than during the follicular phase (when estrogen levels are higher).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
        <a href="#cite_note-33">
         [33]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Among women living closely together, it was once thought that the onsets of menstruation tend to
       <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_synchrony" title="Menstrual synchrony">
        synchronize
       </a>
       . This effect was first described in 1971, and possibly explained by the action of
       <a href="/wiki/Pheromone" title="Pheromone">
        pheromones
       </a>
       in 1998.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mclintock_34-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Mclintock-34">
         [34]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Subsequent research has called this hypothesis into question.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35">
        <a href="#cite_note-35">
         [35]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       As a side note, research indicates that women have a significantly higher likelihood of
       <a href="/wiki/Anterior_cruciate_ligament" title="Anterior cruciate ligament">
        anterior cruciate ligament
       </a>
       injuries in the pre-ovulatory stage, than post-ovulatory stage.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36">
        <a href="#cite_note-36">
         [36]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Fertility">
        Fertility
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Fertility">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Further information:
       <a href="/wiki/Fertility_testing" title="Fertility testing">
        Fertility testing
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pregnancy_chance_by_day_near_ovulation.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1943" data-file-width="2286" height="187" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Pregnancy_chance_by_day_near_ovulation.jpg/220px-Pregnancy_chance_by_day_near_ovulation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Pregnancy_chance_by_day_near_ovulation.jpg/330px-Pregnancy_chance_by_day_near_ovulation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/Pregnancy_chance_by_day_near_ovulation.jpg/440px-Pregnancy_chance_by_day_near_ovulation.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pregnancy_chance_by_day_near_ovulation.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Chance of
         <a href="/wiki/Human_fertilization" title="Human fertilization">
          fertilization
         </a>
         by menstrual cycle day relative to ovulation.
         <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DunsonBaird1999_37-0">
          <a href="#cite_note-DunsonBaird1999-37">
           [37]
          </a>
         </sup>
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The most fertile period (the time with the highest likelihood of pregnancy resulting from
       <a href="/wiki/Sexual_intercourse" title="Sexual intercourse">
        sexual intercourse
       </a>
       ) covers the time from some 5 days before until 1 to 2 days after ovulation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38">
        <a href="#cite_note-38">
         [38]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In a 28‑day cycle with a 14‑day luteal phase, this corresponds to the second and the beginning of the third week. A variety of methods have been developed to help individual women estimate the relatively fertile and the relatively infertile days in the cycle; these systems are called
       <a href="/wiki/Fertility_awareness" title="Fertility awareness">
        fertility awareness
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       Fertility awareness methods that rely on cycle length records alone are called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Calendar-based_methods" title="Calendar-based methods">
        calendar-based methods
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_39-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-who-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Methods that require observation of one or more of the three primary fertility signs (
       <a href="/wiki/Basal_body_temperature" title="Basal body temperature">
        basal body temperature
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cervical_mucus" title="Cervical mucus">
        cervical mucus
       </a>
       , and cervical position)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
        <a href="#cite_note-40">
         [40]
        </a>
       </sup>
       are known as symptoms-based methods.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who_39-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-who-39">
         [39]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urinalysis" title="Urinalysis">
        Urine test
       </a>
       kits are available that detect the LH surge that occurs 24 to 36 hours before ovulation; these are known as ovulation predictor kits (OPKs).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41">
        <a href="#cite_note-41">
         [41]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Computerized devices that interpret basal body temperatures, urinary test results, or changes in saliva are called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fertility_monitors" title="Fertility monitors">
        fertility monitors
       </a>
       .
      </p>
      <p>
       A
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Female_fertility" title="Female fertility">
        woman's fertility
       </a>
       is also affected by her age.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
        <a href="#cite_note-42">
         [42]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As a woman's total egg supply is formed in fetal life,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
        <a href="#cite_note-43">
         [43]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to be ovulated decades later, it has been suggested that this long lifetime may make the
       <a href="/wiki/Chromatin" title="Chromatin">
        chromatin
       </a>
       of eggs more vulnerable to division problems, breakage, and mutation than the chromatin of sperm, which are produced continuously during a man's reproductive life. However, despite this hypothesis, a similar
       <a href="/wiki/Paternal_age_effect" title="Paternal age effect">
        paternal age effect
       </a>
       has also been observed.
      </p>
      <p>
       As measured on women undergoing
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/In_vitro_fertilization" title="In vitro fertilization">
        in vitro fertilization
       </a>
       , a longer menstrual cycle length is associated with higher pregnancy and delivery rates, even after age adjustment.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brodin2008_44-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Brodin2008-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Delivery_rate" title="Delivery rate">
        Delivery rates
       </a>
       after IVF have been estimated to be almost doubled for women with a menstrual cycle length of more than 34 days compared with women with a menstrual cycle length shorter than 26 days.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brodin2008_44-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Brodin2008-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A longer menstrual cycle length is also significantly associated with better ovarian response to
       <a href="/wiki/Gonadotropin" title="Gonadotropin">
        gonadotropin
       </a>
       stimulation and
       <a href="/wiki/Embryo_quality" title="Embryo quality">
        embryo quality
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brodin2008_44-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Brodin2008-44">
         [44]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mood_and_behavior">
        Mood and behavior
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Mood and behavior">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The different phases of the menstrual cycle correlate with women’s
       <a href="/wiki/Mood_(psychology)" title="Mood (psychology)">
        moods
       </a>
       . In some cases, hormones released during the menstrual cycle can cause behavioral changes in females; mild to severe mood changes can occur.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45">
        <a href="#cite_note-45">
         [45]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The menstrual cycle phase and ovarian
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hormones" title="Hormones">
        hormones
       </a>
       may contribute to increased
       <a href="/wiki/Empathy" title="Empathy">
        empathy
       </a>
       in women. The natural shift of hormone levels during the different phases of the menstrual cycle has been studied in conjunction with test scores. When completing empathy exercises, women in the
       <a href="/wiki/Follicular_phase" title="Follicular phase">
        follicular stage
       </a>
       of their menstrual cycle performed better than women in their
       <a href="/wiki/Luteal_phase" title="Luteal phase">
        midluteal phase
       </a>
       . A significant correlation between
       <a href="/wiki/Progesterone" title="Progesterone">
        progesterone
       </a>
       levels and the ability to accurately recognize emotion was found. Performances on
       <a href="/wiki/Emotion_recognition" title="Emotion recognition">
        emotion recognition
       </a>
       tasks were better when women had lower progesterone levels. Women in the follicular stage showed higher emotion recognition accuracy than their midluteal phase counterparts. Women were found to react more to negative stimuli when in midluteal stage over the women in the follicular stage, perhaps indicating more reactivity to social stress during that menstrual cycle phase.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
        <a href="#cite_note-46">
         [46]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Overall, it has been found that women in the follicular phase demonstrated better performance in tasks that contain empathetic traits.
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Fear_processing_in_the_brain" title="Fear processing in the brain">
        Fear response
       </a>
       in women during two different points in the menstrual cycle has been examined. When
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oestrogen" title="Oestrogen">
        estrogen
       </a>
       is highest in the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Preovulatory" title="Preovulatory">
        preovulatory stage
       </a>
       , women are significantly better at identifying expressions of fear than women who were menstruating, which is when estrogen levels are lowest. The women were equally able to identify happy faces, demonstrating that the fear response was a more powerful response. To summarize, menstrual cycle phase and the estrogen levels correlates with women’s fear processing.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
        <a href="#cite_note-47">
         [47]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       However, the examination of daily moods in women with measuring ovarian hormones may indicate a less powerful connection. In comparison to levels of
       <a href="/wiki/Stress_(biology)" title="Stress (biology)">
        stress
       </a>
       or physical health, the ovarian hormones had less of an impact on overall mood.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48">
        <a href="#cite_note-48">
         [48]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This indicates that while changes of ovarian hormones may influence mood, on a day-to-day level it does not influence mood more than other stressors do.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Eating_behavior">
        Eating behavior
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Eating behavior">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Females have been found to experience different eating habits at different stages of their menstrual cycle, with food intake being higher during the
       <a href="/wiki/Luteal_phase" title="Luteal phase">
        luteal phase
       </a>
       than the
       <a href="/wiki/Follicular_phase" title="Follicular phase">
        follicular phase
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_49-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_50-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Food intake increases by approximately 10% during the luteal phase compared to the follicular phase.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_50-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Various studies have shown that during the luteal phase woman consume more
       <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">
        carbohydrates
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">
        proteins
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">
        fats
       </a>
       and that 24-hour
       <a href="/wiki/Energy_homeostasis" title="Energy homeostasis">
        energy expenditure
       </a>
       shows increases between 2.5-11.5%.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_51-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:02-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The increasing intake during the luteal phase may be related to higher preferences for sweet and fatty foods, which occurs naturally and is enhanced during the luteal phases of the menstrual cycle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_51-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:02-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This is due to the higher metabolic demand during this phase.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_52-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:03-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In particular, women tend to show a cravings for chocolate, with higher cravings during the luteal phase.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:02_51-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:02-51">
         [51]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Females with
       <a href="/wiki/Premenstrual_syndrome" title="Premenstrual syndrome">
        premenstrual syndrome
       </a>
       (PMS) report changes in appetite across the menstrual cycle more than non-sufferers of PMS, possibly due to their oversensitivity to changes in hormone levels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_50-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In women with PMS, food intake is higher in the
       <a href="/wiki/Luteal_phase" title="Luteal phase">
        luteal phase
       </a>
       than
       <a href="/wiki/Follicular_phase" title="Follicular phase">
        follicular
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_53-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:3-53">
         [53]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The remaining symptoms of PMS, including mood changes and physical symptoms, also occur during the luteal phase. No difference for preference of food types has been found between PMS sufferers and non-sufferers.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_49-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The different levels of
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovarian_hormones" title="Ovarian hormones">
        ovarian hormones
       </a>
       at different stages of the cycle have been used to explain eating behaviour changes. Progesterone has been shown to promote fat storage, causing a higher intake of fatty foods during the luteal phase when progesterone levels are higher.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_50-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Additionally, with a high
       <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">
        estrogen
       </a>
       level dopamine is ineffective in converting to
       <a href="/wiki/Norepinephrine" title="Norepinephrine">
        noradrenaline
       </a>
       , a hormone which promotes eating, therefore decreasing appetite.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_50-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In humans, the level of these ovarian hormones during the menstrual cycle have been found to influence
       <a href="/wiki/Binge_eating" title="Binge eating">
        binge eating
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
        <a href="#cite_note-54">
         [54]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       It is theorized that the use of
       <a href="/wiki/Oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Oral contraceptive pill">
        birth control pills
       </a>
       should affect eating behaviour as they minimise or remove the fluctuations in hormone levels.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_49-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:0-49">
         [49]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The
       <a href="/wiki/Neurotransmitter" title="Neurotransmitter">
        neurotransmitter
       </a>
       <a href="/wiki/Serotonin" title="Serotonin">
        serotonin
       </a>
       is also thought to play a role in food intake. Serotonin is responsible for inhibiting eating and controlling meal size,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55">
        <a href="#cite_note-55">
         [55]
        </a>
       </sup>
       among other things, and is modulated in part by ovarian hormones.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
        <a href="#cite_note-56">
         [56]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       A number of factors affect whether dieting will affect these menstrual processes: age, weight loss and the diet itself. First, younger women are likely to experience
       <a href="/wiki/Irregular_menstruation" title="Irregular menstruation">
        menstrual irregularities
       </a>
       due to their diet. Second, menstrual abnormalities are more likely with more weight loss. For example,
       <a href="/wiki/Anovulatory_cycle" title="Anovulatory cycle">
        anovulatory cycles
       </a>
       can occur as a result of adopting a restricted diet, as well as engaging in a high amount of exercise.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_50-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-:4-50">
         [50]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Finally, the cycle is affected more by a vegetarian diet compared to a non-vegetarian diet.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:22_57-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:22-57">
         [57]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Substance_abuse">
        Substance abuse
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Substance abuse">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       Studies investigating effects of the menstrual cycle on alcohol consumption have found mixed evidence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:9_58-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-:9-58">
         [58]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, some evidence suggests that individuals consume more alcohol during the luteal stage, especially if these individuals are heavy drinkers or have a family history of
       <a href="/wiki/Alcohol_abuse" title="Alcohol abuse">
        alcohol abuse
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_52-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-:03-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The level of substance abuse increases with PMS, mostly with addictive substances such as
       <a href="/wiki/Nicotine" title="Nicotine">
        nicotine
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Tobacco" title="Tobacco">
        tobacco
       </a>
       and
       <a href="/wiki/Cocaine" title="Cocaine">
        cocaine
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_52-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-:03-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
       One theory behind this suggests this higher level of substance abuse is due to decreased self-control as a result of the higher metabolic demands during the luteal phase.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:03_52-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-:03-52">
         [52]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Menstrual_disorders">
        Menstrual disorders
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Menstrual disorders">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_disorder" title="Menstrual disorder">
        Menstrual disorder
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Infrequent or irregular ovulation is called
       <i>
        oligoovulation
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
        <a href="#cite_note-59">
         [59]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The absence of ovulation is called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Anovulation" title="Anovulation">
         anovulation
        </a>
       </i>
       . Normal menstrual flow can occur without ovulation preceding it: an
       <a href="/wiki/Anovulatory_cycle" title="Anovulatory cycle">
        anovulatory cycle
       </a>
       . In some cycles, follicular development may start but not be completed; nevertheless, estrogens will be formed and stimulate the uterine lining. Anovulatory flow resulting from a very thick endometrium caused by prolonged, continued high estrogen levels is called
       <i>
        estrogen breakthrough bleeding
       </i>
       . Anovulatory bleeding triggered by a sudden drop in estrogen levels is called
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Withdrawal_bleeding" title="Withdrawal bleeding">
        withdrawal bleeding
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tcoyf3_60-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tcoyf3-60">
         [60]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Anovulatory cycles commonly occur before
       <a href="/wiki/Menopause" title="Menopause">
        menopause
       </a>
       (perimenopause) and in women with
       <a href="/wiki/Polycystic_ovary_syndrome" title="Polycystic ovary syndrome">
        polycystic ovary syndrome
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emed2_61-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-emed2-61">
         [61]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Very little flow (less than 10 ml) is called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Hypomenorrhea" title="Hypomenorrhea">
         hypomenorrhea
        </a>
       </i>
       . Regular cycles with intervals of 21 days or fewer are
       <i>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polymenorrhea" title="Polymenorrhea">
         polymenorrhea
        </a>
       </i>
       ; frequent but irregular menstruation is known as
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Metrorrhagia" title="Metrorrhagia">
         metrorrhagia
        </a>
       </i>
       . Sudden heavy flows or amounts greater than 80 ml are termed
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Menorrhagia" title="Menorrhagia">
         menorrhagia
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emed1_62-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-emed1-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Heavy menstruation that occurs frequently and irregularly is
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Menometrorrhagia" title="Menometrorrhagia">
         menometrorrhagia
        </a>
       </i>
       . The term for cycles with intervals exceeding 35 days is
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Oligomenorrhea" title="Oligomenorrhea">
         oligomenorrhea
        </a>
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afp_63-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-afp-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <a href="/wiki/Amenorrhea" title="Amenorrhea">
        Amenorrhea
       </a>
       refers to more than three
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-emed1_62-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-emed1-62">
         [62]
        </a>
       </sup>
       to six
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-afp_63-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-afp-63">
         [63]
        </a>
       </sup>
       months without menses (while not being pregnant) during a woman's reproductive years.
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Cycles_and_phases">
        Cycles and phases
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cycles and phases">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MenstrualCycle3_en.svg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="252" data-file-width="435" height="203" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/MenstrualCycle3_en.svg/350px-MenstrualCycle3_en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/MenstrualCycle3_en.svg/525px-MenstrualCycle3_en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/04/MenstrualCycle3_en.svg/700px-MenstrualCycle3_en.svg.png 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MenstrualCycle3_en.svg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Menstrual cycle
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Menstrual_cycle_diagram.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="396" data-file-width="864" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Menstrual_cycle_diagram.png/350px-Menstrual_cycle_diagram.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Menstrual_cycle_diagram.png/525px-Menstrual_cycle_diagram.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/Menstrual_cycle_diagram.png/700px-Menstrual_cycle_diagram.png 2x" width="350"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Menstrual_cycle_diagram.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Flowchart of the hormonal control of the menstrual cycle
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       The menstrual cycle can be described by the ovarian or uterine cycle. The ovarian cycle describes changes that occur in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_follicle" title="Ovarian follicle">
        follicles
       </a>
       of the ovary whereas the uterine cycle describes changes in the
       <a href="/wiki/Endometrium" title="Endometrium">
        endometrial lining
       </a>
       of the uterus. Both cycles can be divided into three phases. The ovarian cycle consists of the follicular phase, ovulation, and the luteal phase whereas the uterine cycle consists of menstruation, proliferative phase, and secretory phase.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverthorn_1-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-Silverthorn-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ovarian_cycle">
        Ovarian cycle
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Ovarian cycle">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Follicular_phase">
        Follicular phase
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Follicular phase">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Follicular_phase" title="Follicular phase">
        Follicular phase
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The follicular phase is the first part of the ovarian cycle. During this phase, the ovarian follicles mature and get ready to release an egg.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Silverthorn_1-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-Silverthorn-1">
         [1]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The latter part of this phase overlaps with the proliferative phase of the uterine cycle.
      </p>
      <p>
       Through the influence of a rise in
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Follicle_stimulating_hormone" title="Follicle stimulating hormone">
        follicle stimulating hormone
       </a>
       (FSH) during the first days of the cycle, a few
       <a href="/wiki/Ovarian_follicle" title="Ovarian follicle">
        ovarian follicles
       </a>
       are stimulated.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-303120-8_64-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-303120-8-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       These follicles, which were present at birth
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-303120-8_64-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-303120-8-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       and have been developing for the better part of a year in a process known as
       <a href="/wiki/Folliculogenesis" title="Folliculogenesis">
        folliculogenesis
       </a>
       , compete with each other for dominance. Under the influence of several hormones, all but one of these follicles will stop growing, while one dominant follicle in the ovary will continue to maturity. The follicle that reaches maturity is called a tertiary, or Graafian, follicle, and it contains the ovum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-303120-8_64-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-303120-8-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ovulation">
        Ovulation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Ovulation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Ovulation" title="Ovulation">
        Ovulation
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ovulation.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="973" data-file-width="688" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ovulation.jpg/220px-Ovulation.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ovulation.jpg/330px-Ovulation.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/Ovulation.jpg/440px-Ovulation.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ovulation.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         An ovary about to release an egg
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       Ovulation is the second phase of the ovarian cycle in which a mature egg is released from the ovarian follicles into the oviduct.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
        <a href="#cite_note-65">
         [65]
        </a>
       </sup>
       During the follicular phase,
       <a href="/wiki/Estradiol" title="Estradiol">
        estradiol
       </a>
       suppresses release of
       <a href="/wiki/Luteinizing_hormone" title="Luteinizing hormone">
        luteinizing hormone
       </a>
       (LH) from the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anterior_pituitary_gland" title="Anterior pituitary gland">
        anterior pituitary gland
       </a>
       . When the egg has nearly matured, levels of
       <a href="/wiki/Estradiol" title="Estradiol">
        estradiol
       </a>
       reach a threshold above which this effect is reversed and estrogen stimulates the production of a large amount of LH. This process, known as the LH surge, starts around day 12 of the average cycle and may last 48 hours.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
        <a href="#cite_note-66">
         [66]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The exact mechanism of these opposite responses of LH levels to estradiol is not well understood.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lentz_67-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lentz-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       In animals, a
       <a href="/wiki/Gonadotropin-releasing_hormone" title="Gonadotropin-releasing hormone">
        gonadotropin-releasing hormone
       </a>
       (GnRH) surge has been shown to precede the LH surge, suggesting that estrogen's main effect is on the hypothalamus, which controls GnRH secretion.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lentz_67-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lentz-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       This may be enabled by the presence of two different estrogen receptors in the hypothalamus:
       <a href="/wiki/Estrogen_receptor_alpha" title="Estrogen receptor alpha">
        estrogen receptor alpha
       </a>
       , which is responsible for the negative feedback estradiol-LH loop, and
       <a href="/wiki/Estrogen_receptor_beta" title="Estrogen receptor beta">
        estrogen receptor beta
       </a>
       , which is responsible for the positive estradiol-LH relationship.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
        <a href="#cite_note-68">
         [68]
        </a>
       </sup>
       However, in humans it has been shown that high levels of estradiol can provoke abrupt increases in LH, even when GnRH levels and pulse frequencies are held constant,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lentz_67-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Lentz-67">
         [67]
        </a>
       </sup>
       suggesting that estrogen acts directly on the pituitary to provoke the LH surge.
      </p>
      <p>
       The release of LH matures the egg and weakens the wall of the follicle in the ovary, causing the fully developed follicle to release its
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Secondary_oocyte" title="Secondary oocyte">
        secondary oocyte
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-303120-8_64-3">
        <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-303120-8-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The secondary oocyte promptly matures into an
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ootid" title="Ootid">
        ootid
       </a>
       and then becomes a mature
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ovum" title="Ovum">
        ovum
       </a>
       . The mature ovum has a diameter of about 0.2 mm.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69">
        <a href="#cite_note-69">
         [69]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Which of the two ovaries—left or right—ovulates appears essentially random; no known left and right co-ordination exists.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ov_70-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-ov-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Occasionally, both ovaries will release an egg;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ov_70-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-ov-70">
         [70]
        </a>
       </sup>
       if both eggs are fertilized, the result is
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fraternal_twin" title="Fraternal twin">
        fraternal twins
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-twins_71-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-twins-71">
         [71]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       After being released from the ovary, the egg is swept into the
       <a href="/wiki/Fallopian_tube" title="Fallopian tube">
        fallopian tube
       </a>
       by the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fimbria_(female_reproductive_system)" title="Fimbria (female reproductive system)">
        fimbria
       </a>
       , which is a fringe of tissue at the end of each fallopian tube. After about a day, an unfertilized egg will disintegrate or dissolve in the fallopian tube.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-303120-8_64-4">
        <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-303120-8-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Fertilization by a
       <a href="/wiki/Spermatozoon" title="Spermatozoon">
        spermatozoon
       </a>
       , when it occurs, usually takes place in the
       <a href="/wiki/Ampulla_of_uterine_tube" title="Ampulla of uterine tube">
        ampulla
       </a>
       , the widest section of the fallopian tubes. A fertilized egg immediately begins the process of
       <a href="/wiki/Human_embryogenesis" title="Human embryogenesis">
        embryogenesis
       </a>
       , or development. The developing embryo takes about three days to reach the uterus and another three days to implant into the endometrium.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-303120-8_64-5">
        <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-303120-8-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       It has usually reached the
       <a href="/wiki/Blastocyst" title="Blastocyst">
        blastocyst
       </a>
       stage at the time of implantation.
      </p>
      <p>
       In some women, ovulation features a characteristic pain called
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Mittelschmerz" title="Mittelschmerz">
         mittelschmerz
        </a>
       </i>
       (German term meaning
       <i>
        middle pain
       </i>
       ).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmenses_72-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-allmenses-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The sudden change in hormones at the time of ovulation sometimes also causes light mid-cycle blood flow.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
        <a href="#cite_note-73">
         [73]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Luteal_phase">
        Luteal phase
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Luteal phase">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Luteal_phase" title="Luteal phase">
        Luteal phase
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       The luteal phase is the final phase of the ovarian cycle and it corresponds to the secretory phase of the uterine cycle. During the luteal phase, the
       <a href="/wiki/Pituitary_gland" title="Pituitary gland">
        pituitary hormones
       </a>
       FSH and LH cause the remaining parts of the dominant follicle to transform into the corpus luteum, which produces progesterone. The increased progesterone in the adrenals starts to induce the production of estrogen. The hormones produced by the corpus luteum also suppress production of the FSH and LH that the corpus luteum needs to maintain itself. Consequently, the level of FSH and LH fall quickly over time, and the corpus luteum subsequently atrophies.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-303120-8_64-6">
        <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-303120-8-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Falling levels of progesterone trigger menstruation and the beginning of the next cycle. From the time of ovulation until progesterone withdrawal has caused menstruation to begin, the process typically takes about two weeks, with 14 days considered normal. For an individual woman, the follicular phase often varies in length from cycle to cycle; by contrast, the length of her luteal phase will be fairly consistent from cycle to cycle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
        <a href="#cite_note-74">
         [74]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The loss of the corpus luteum is prevented by fertilization of the egg. The
       <a href="/wiki/Syncytiotrophoblast" title="Syncytiotrophoblast">
        syncytiotrophoblast
       </a>
       , which is the outer layer of the resulting embryo-containing structure (the
       <a href="/wiki/Blastocyst" title="Blastocyst">
        blastocyst
       </a>
       ) and later also becomes the outer layer of the placenta, produces
       <a href="/wiki/Human_chorionic_gonadotropin" title="Human chorionic gonadotropin">
        human chorionic gonadotropin
       </a>
       (hCG), which is very similar to LH and which preserves the corpus luteum. The corpus luteum can then continue to secrete progesterone to maintain the new pregnancy. Most
       <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy_test" title="Pregnancy test">
        pregnancy tests
       </a>
       look for the presence of hCG.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-303120-8_64-7">
        <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-303120-8-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Uterine_cycle">
        Uterine cycle
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Uterine cycle">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The uterine cycle has three phases: menses, proliferative, secretory.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75">
        <a href="#cite_note-75">
         [75]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Menstruation">
        Menstruation
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Menstruation">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">
        Menstruation
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Menstruation (also called menstrual bleeding, menses, catamenia or a period) is the first phase of the uterine cycle. The flow of menses normally serves as a sign that a woman has not become
       <a href="/wiki/Pregnancy" title="Pregnancy">
        pregnant
       </a>
       . (However, this cannot be taken as certainty, as a number of factors can cause
       <a href="/wiki/Vaginal_bleeding#Pregnant_women" title="Vaginal bleeding">
        bleeding during pregnancy
       </a>
       ; some factors are specific to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/First_trimester_bleeding" title="First trimester bleeding">
        early pregnancy
       </a>
       , and some can cause
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Obstetrical_hemorrhage" title="Obstetrical hemorrhage">
        heavy flow
       </a>
       .)
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
        <a href="#cite_note-76">
         [76]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
        <a href="#cite_note-77">
         [77]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
        <a href="#cite_note-78">
         [78]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hormones_estradiol,_progesterone,_LH_and_FSH_during_menstrual_cycle.png">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3634" data-file-width="1846" height="433" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Hormones_estradiol%2C_progesterone%2C_LH_and_FSH_during_menstrual_cycle.png/220px-Hormones_estradiol%2C_progesterone%2C_LH_and_FSH_during_menstrual_cycle.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Hormones_estradiol%2C_progesterone%2C_LH_and_FSH_during_menstrual_cycle.png/330px-Hormones_estradiol%2C_progesterone%2C_LH_and_FSH_during_menstrual_cycle.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Hormones_estradiol%2C_progesterone%2C_LH_and_FSH_during_menstrual_cycle.png/440px-Hormones_estradiol%2C_progesterone%2C_LH_and_FSH_during_menstrual_cycle.png 2x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hormones_estradiol,_progesterone,_LH_and_FSH_during_menstrual_cycle.png" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Levels of
         <a href="/wiki/Estradiol" title="Estradiol">
          estradiol
         </a>
         (the main estrogen),
         <a href="/wiki/Progesterone" title="Progesterone">
          progesterone
         </a>
         , luteinizing hormone, and
         <a href="/wiki/Follicle-stimulating_hormone" title="Follicle-stimulating hormone">
          follicle-stimulating hormone
         </a>
         during the menstrual cycle, taking inter-cycle and inter-woman variability into account.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       <i>
        Eumenorrhea
       </i>
       denotes normal, regular menstruation that lasts for a few days (usually 3 to 5 days, but anywhere from 2 to 7 days is considered normal).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmenses_72-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-allmenses-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-US-typical_79-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-US-typical-79">
         [79]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The average
       <a href="/wiki/Bleeding" title="Bleeding">
        blood loss
       </a>
       during menstruation is 35 milliliters with 10–80 ml considered normal.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bloodloss_80-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-bloodloss-80">
         [80]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Women who experience
       <a href="/wiki/Menorrhagia" title="Menorrhagia">
        Menorrhagia
       </a>
       are more susceptible to
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_deficiency_(medicine)" title="Iron deficiency (medicine)">
        iron deficiency
       </a>
       than the average person.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-iron_81-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-iron-81">
         [81]
        </a>
       </sup>
       An
       <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">
        enzyme
       </a>
       called
       <a href="/wiki/Plasmin" title="Plasmin">
        plasmin
       </a>
       inhibits
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_clotting" title="Blood clotting">
        clotting
       </a>
       in the menstrual fluid.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-plasmin_82-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-plasmin-82">
         [82]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Painful cramping in the abdomen, back, or upper thighs is common during the first few days of menstruation. Severe uterine pain during menstruation is known as
       <a href="/wiki/Dysmenorrhea" title="Dysmenorrhea">
        dysmenorrhea
       </a>
       , and it is most common among adolescents and younger women (affecting about 67.2% of adolescent females).
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
        <a href="#cite_note-83">
         [83]
        </a>
       </sup>
       When menstruation begins, symptoms of
       <a href="/wiki/Premenstrual_syndrome" title="Premenstrual syndrome">
        premenstrual syndrome
       </a>
       (PMS) such as
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mastalgia" title="Mastalgia">
        breast tenderness
       </a>
       and irritability generally decrease.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-allmenses_72-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-allmenses-72">
         [72]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Many
       <a href="/wiki/Menstruation#Menstrual_products" title="Menstruation">
        sanitary products
       </a>
       are marketed to women for use during their menstruation.
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Proliferative_phase">
        Proliferative phase
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Proliferative phase">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The proliferative phase is the second phase of the uterine cycle when estrogen causes the lining of the uterus to grow, or proliferate, during this time.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-isbn0-07-303120-8_64-8">
        <a href="#cite_note-isbn0-07-303120-8-64">
         [64]
        </a>
       </sup>
       As they mature, the ovarian follicles secrete increasing amounts of
       <a href="/wiki/Estradiol" title="Estradiol">
        estradiol
       </a>
       , and
       <a href="/wiki/Estrogen" title="Estrogen">
        estrogen
       </a>
       . The estrogens initiate the formation of a new layer of
       <a href="/wiki/Endometrium" title="Endometrium">
        endometrium
       </a>
       in the uterus, histologically identified as the proliferative endometrium. The estrogen also stimulates
       <a href="/wiki/Crypt_(anatomy)" title="Crypt (anatomy)">
        crypts
       </a>
       in the
       <a href="/wiki/Cervix" title="Cervix">
        cervix
       </a>
       to produce fertile cervical mucus, which may be noticed by women practicing
       <a href="/wiki/Fertility_awareness" title="Fertility awareness">
        fertility awareness
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tcoyf2_84-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tcoyf2-84">
         [84]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h4>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Secretory_phase">
        Secretory phase
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Secretory phase">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h4>
      <p>
       The secretory phase is the final phase of the uterine cycle and it corresponds to the luteal phase of the ovarian cycle. During the secretory phase, the corpus luteum produces progesterone, which plays a vital role in making the
       <a href="/wiki/Endometrium" title="Endometrium">
        endometrium
       </a>
       receptive to
       <a href="/wiki/Implantation_(human_embryo)" title="Implantation (human embryo)">
        implantation
       </a>
       of the
       <a href="/wiki/Blastocyst" title="Blastocyst">
        blastocyst
       </a>
       and supportive of the early pregnancy, by increasing blood flow and uterine secretions and reducing the contractility of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Smooth_muscle" title="Smooth muscle">
        smooth muscle
       </a>
       in the uterus;
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
        <a href="#cite_note-85">
         [85]
        </a>
       </sup>
       it also has the side effect of raising the woman's
       <a href="/wiki/Basal_body_temperature" title="Basal body temperature">
        basal body temperature
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tcoyf_86-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-tcoyf-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Ovulation_suppression">
        Ovulation suppression
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Ovulation suppression">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Birth_control">
        Birth control
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Birth control">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a href="/wiki/Hormonal_contraception" title="Hormonal contraception">
        Hormonal contraception
       </a>
      </div>
      <div class="thumb tright">
       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Pillpacketopen.jpg">
         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="170" data-file-width="240" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f5/Pillpacketopen.jpg/220px-Pillpacketopen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/f5/Pillpacketopen.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/>
        </a>
        <div class="thumbcaption">
         <div class="magnify">
          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Pillpacketopen.jpg" title="Enlarge">
          </a>
         </div>
         Half-used blister pack of a combined oral contraceptive. The white pills are
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Placebos" title="Placebos">
          placebos
         </a>
         , mainly for the purpose of reminding the woman to continue taking the pills.
        </div>
       </div>
      </div>
      <p>
       While some forms of
       <a href="/wiki/Birth_control" title="Birth control">
        birth control
       </a>
       do not affect the menstrual cycle, hormonal contraceptives work by disrupting it. Progestogen
       <a href="/wiki/Negative_feedback" title="Negative feedback">
        negative feedback
       </a>
       decreases the pulse frequency of
       <a href="/wiki/Gonadotropin-releasing_hormone" title="Gonadotropin-releasing hormone">
        gonadotropin-releasing hormone
       </a>
       (GnRH) release by the
