       <a href="/wiki/Hypothalamus" title="Hypothalamus">
        hypothalamus
       </a>
       , which decreases the release of
       <a href="/wiki/Follicle-stimulating_hormone" title="Follicle-stimulating hormone">
        follicle-stimulating hormone
       </a>
       (FSH) and luteinizing hormone (LH) by the
       <a href="/wiki/Anterior_pituitary" title="Anterior pituitary">
        anterior pituitary
       </a>
       . Decreased levels of FSH inhibit follicular development, preventing an increase in
       <a href="/wiki/Estradiol" title="Estradiol">
        estradiol
       </a>
       levels. Progestogen negative feedback and the lack of estrogen
       <a href="/wiki/Positive_feedback" title="Positive feedback">
        positive feedback
       </a>
       on LH release prevent a mid-cycle LH surge. Inhibition of follicular development and the absence of a LH surge prevent ovulation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hatcher_87-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-hatcher-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-speroff_88-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-speroff-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loose_89-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-loose-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       The degree of ovulation suppression in progestogen-only contraceptives depends on the
       <a href="/wiki/Progestogen" title="Progestogen">
        progestogen
       </a>
       activity and dose. Low dose progestogen-only contraceptives—traditional
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Progestogen_only_pill" title="Progestogen only pill">
        progestogen only pills
       </a>
       ,
       <a href="/wiki/Subdermal_implant" title="Subdermal implant">
        subdermal implants
       </a>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norplant" title="Norplant">
        Norplant
       </a>
       and Jadelle, and
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IntraUterine_System" title="IntraUterine System">
        intrauterine system
       </a>
       Mirena—inhibit ovulation in about 50% of cycles and rely mainly on other effects, such as thickening of cervical mucus, for their contraceptive effectiveness.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glasier_90-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-glasier-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Intermediate dose progestogen-only contraceptives—the progestogen-only pill Cerazette and the subdermal implant
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nexplanon" title="Nexplanon">
        Nexplanon
       </a>
       —allow some follicular development but more consistently inhibit ovulation in 97–99% of cycles. The same cervical mucus changes occur as with very low-dose progestogens. High-dose, progestogen-only contraceptives—the injectables
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Depo-Provera" title="Depo-Provera">
        Depo-Provera
       </a>
       and Noristerat—completely inhibit follicular development and ovulation.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-glasier_90-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-glasier-90">
         [90]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       Combined hormonal contraceptives include both an estrogen and a progestogen. Estrogen negative feedback on the anterior pituitary greatly decreases the release of FSH, which makes combined hormonal contraceptives more effective at inhibiting follicular development and preventing ovulation. Estrogen also reduces the incidence of irregular
       <a href="/wiki/Breakthrough_bleeding" title="Breakthrough bleeding">
        breakthrough bleeding
       </a>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-hatcher_87-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-hatcher-87">
         [87]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-speroff_88-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-speroff-88">
         [88]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-loose_89-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-loose-89">
         [89]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Several combined hormonal contraceptives—
       <a href="/wiki/Combined_oral_contraceptive_pill" title="Combined oral contraceptive pill">
        the pill
       </a>
       ,
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NuvaRing" title="NuvaRing">
        NuvaRing
       </a>
       , and the
       <a href="/wiki/Contraceptive_patch" title="Contraceptive patch">
        contraceptive patch
       </a>
       —are usually used in a way that causes regular
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Withdrawal_bleeding" title="Withdrawal bleeding">
        withdrawal bleeding
       </a>
       . In a normal cycle,
       <a href="/wiki/Menstruation" title="Menstruation">
        menstruation
       </a>
       occurs when estrogen and progesterone levels drop rapidly.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-tcoyf_86-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-tcoyf-86">
         [86]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Temporarily discontinuing use of combined hormonal contraceptives (a placebo week, not using patch or ring for a week) has a similar effect of causing the uterine lining to shed. If withdrawal bleeding is not desired, combined hormonal contraceptives may be
       <a href="/wiki/Extended_cycle_combined_hormonal_contraceptive" title="Extended cycle combined hormonal contraceptive">
        taken continuously
       </a>
       , although this increases the risk of breakthrough bleeding.
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Breastfeeding">
        Breastfeeding
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Breastfeeding">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       Main article:
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lactational_amenorrhea_method" title="Lactational amenorrhea method">
        Lactational amenorrhea method
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Breastfeeding" title="Breastfeeding">
        Breastfeeding
       </a>
       causes negative feedback to occur on pulse secretion of gonadotropin-releasing hormone (GnRH) and luteinizing hormone (LH). Depending on the strength of the negative feedback, breastfeeding women may experience complete suppression of follicular development, but no ovulation, or normal menstrual cycle may resume.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcneilly_91-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-mcneilly-91">
         [91]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Suppression of ovulation is more likely when suckling occurs more frequently.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_92-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-92">
         [92]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The production of
       <a href="/wiki/Prolactin" title="Prolactin">
        prolactin
       </a>
       in response to suckling is important to maintaining lactational amenorrhea.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93">
        <a href="#cite_note-93">
         [93]
        </a>
       </sup>
       On average, women who are fully breastfeeding whose infants suckle frequently experience a return of menstruation at fourteen and a half months postpartum. There is a wide range of response among individual breastfeeding women, however, with some experiencing return of menstruation at two months and others remaining amenorrheic for up to 42 months postpartum.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94">
        <a href="#cite_note-94">
         [94]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h2>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">
        Society and culture
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Society and culture">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h2>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Culture_and_menstruation" title="Culture and menstruation">
        Culture and menstruation
       </a>
      </div>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymological">
        Etymological
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Etymological">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       The word "menstruation" is etymologically related to "moon". The terms "menstruation" and "menses" are derived from the
       <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">
        Latin
       </a>
       <i>
        mensis
       </i>
       (month), which in turn relates to the
       <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">
        Greek
       </a>
       <i>
        mene
       </i>
       (moon) and to the roots of the English words
       <i>
        month
       </i>
       and
       <i>
        moon
       </i>
       .
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen,_Kevin_2007_239_95-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Allen,_Kevin_2007_239-95">
         [95]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="The_Moon">
        The Moon
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: The Moon">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">
       See also:
       <a href="/wiki/Lunar_effect" title="Lunar effect">
        Lunar effect
       </a>
      </div>
      <p>
       Even though the average length of the human menstrual cycle is similar to that of the
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lunar_cycle" title="Lunar cycle">
        lunar cycle
       </a>
       , in modern humans there is no relation between the two.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96">
        <a href="#cite_note-96">
         [96]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The relationship is believed to be a coincidence.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97">
        <a href="#cite_note-97">
         [97]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98">
        <a href="#cite_note-98">
         [98]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Light exposure does not appear to affect the menstrual cycle in humans.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kris2013_10-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Kris2013-10">
         [10]
        </a>
       </sup>
       A meta-analysis of studies from 1996 showed no correlation between the human menstrual cycle and the lunar cycle.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99">
        <a href="#cite_note-99">
         [99]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100">
        <a href="#cite_note-100">
         [100]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-kelly_101-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-kelly-101">
         [101]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <p>
       <a href="/wiki/Dogon_people" title="Dogon people">
        Dogon villagers
       </a>
       did not have electric lighting and spent most nights outdoors, talking and sleeping; so they were apparently an ideal population for detecting a lunar influence; none was found.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102">
        <a href="#cite_note-102">
         [102]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
      <h3>
       <span class="mw-headline" id="Work">
        Work
       </span>
       <span class="mw-editsection">
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         [
        </span>
        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Menstrual_cycle&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Work">
         edit
        </a>
        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">
         ]
        </span>
       </span>
      </h3>
      <p>
       In a number of countries, mainly in Asia, legislation or corporate practice has introduced formal
       <a href="/wiki/Menstrual_leave" title="Menstrual leave">
        menstrual leave
       </a>
       to provide women with either paid or unpaid leave of absence from their employment while they are menstruating.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic2014_103-0">
        <a href="#cite_note-Atlantic2014-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       Countries with policies include Japan, Taiwan, Indonesia, and South Korea.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic2014_103-1">
        <a href="#cite_note-Atlantic2014-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       The practice is controversial due to concerns that it bolsters the perception of women as weak, inefficient workers,
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Atlantic2014_103-2">
        <a href="#cite_note-Atlantic2014-103">
         [103]
        </a>
       </sup>
       as well as concerns that it is unfair to men.
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104">
        <a href="#cite_note-104">
         [104]
        </a>
       </sup>
       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105">
        <a href="#cite_note-105">
         [105]
        </a>
       </sup>
      </p>
